Borisovich Zel\'dovich">Zel\'dovich</a></li>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#ddd8e7;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology#Subject_history" title="Physical cosmology">Subject history</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Discovery of cosmic microwave background radiation">Discovery of cosmic microwave<br/>background radiation</a></div></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="History of the Big Bang theory">History of the Big Bang theory</a></li>\n<li><div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Religious_interpretations_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="Religious interpretations of the Big Bang theory">Religious interpretations of<br/>the Big Bang theory</a></div></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_cosmological_theories" title="Timeline of cosmological theories">Timeline of cosmological theories</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;display:block;margin-top:0.4em; line-height:1.6em;padding-bottom:0.5em;">\n<ul><li><img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Category:Physical_cosmology" title="Category:Physical cosmology">Category</a></li>\n<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png"><img alt="Ilc 9yr moll4096.png" class="noviewer" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/16px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/24px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/32px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Cosmology" title="Portal:Cosmology">Cosmology portal</a></li>\n<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crab_Nebula.jpg"><img alt="Crab Nebula.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="3864" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/16px-Crab_Nebula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/24px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/32px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Astronomy" title="Portal:Astronomy">Astronomy portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Physical_cosmology" title="Template talk:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Hubble\'s law</b>, also known as the <b>Hubble&#8211;Lema&#238;tre law</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is the observation in <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">physical cosmology</a> that:\n</p>\n<ol><li>Objects observed in <a href="/wiki/Extragalactic_astronomy" title="Extragalactic astronomy">deep space</a>&#8212;extragalactic space, 10 mega<a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> (Mpc) or more&#8212;are found to have a <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>, interpreted as a relative velocity away from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>;</li>\n<li>This <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler shift</a>-measured velocity of various <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> receding from the Earth is approximately <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportional</a> to their distance from the Earth for galaxies up to a few hundred megaparsecs away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riess99_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-riess99-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perlmutter99_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-perlmutter99-3">[3]</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>Hubble\'s law is considered the first observational basis for the <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">expansion of the universe</a> and today serves as one of the pieces of evidence most often cited in support of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170220_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170220-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coles_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coles-5">[5]</a></sup>\nThe motion of astronomical objects due solely to this expansion is known as the <b>Hubble flow</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although widely attributed to <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> the law was first derived from the <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> equations in 1922 by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedmann" title="Alexander Friedmann">Alexander Friedmann</a>.  Friedmann published a set of equations, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations" title="Friedmann equations">Friedmann equations</a>, showing that the universe might expand, and presenting the expansion speed if this was the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>  Then <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a>, in a 1927 article, proposed the expansion of the universe and suggested an estimated value of the rate of expansion, which when corrected by Hubble became known as the <b>Hubble constant</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170220_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170220-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Livio_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Livio-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, though the Hubble constant <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43910602a221b7a4c373791f94793e3008622070" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.985ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is roughly constant in the velocity-distance space at this moment in time, the <b>Hubble parameter</b> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, which the Hubble constant is the current value of, changes with time, so the term \'constant\' is sometimes thought of as somewhat of a misnomer. Moreover, two years later Edwin Hubble confirmed the existence of cosmic expansion, and determined a more accurate value for the constant that now bears his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\nHubble inferred the recession velocity of the objects from their <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifts</a>, many of which were earlier measured and related to velocity by <a href="/wiki/Vesto_Slipher" title="Vesto Slipher">Vesto Slipher</a> in 1917.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MS_Longair_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-MS_Longair-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In October 2018, scientists presented a new third way (two earlier methods gave problematic results that do not agree), using information from <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational wave</a> events (especially those involving the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star_merger" title="Neutron star merger">merger of neutron stars</a>, like <a href="/wiki/GW170817" title="GW170817">GW170817</a>), of determining the Hubble Constant, essential in establishing the exact rate of <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">expansion of the universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20181022_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20181022-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20181017_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20181017-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The law is often expressed by the equation <span class="nowrap"><i>v</i> = <i>H</i><sub>0</sub><i>D</i></span>, with <i>H</i><sub>0</sub> the constant of proportionality&#8212;Hubble constant&#8212;between the "proper distance" <i>D</i> to a galaxy, which can change over time, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Comoving_and_proper_distances" title="Comoving and proper distances">comoving distance</a>, and its velocity <i>v</i>, i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Derivative" title="Derivative">derivative</a> of proper distance with respect to cosmological time coordinate. (See <i><a href="/wiki/Comoving_and_proper_distances#Uses_of_the_proper_distance" title="Comoving and proper distances">uses of the proper distance</a></i> for some discussion of the subtleties of this definition of \'velocity\'.) Also, the SI unit of <i>H</i><sub>0</sub> is s<sup>&#8722;1</sup>, but it is most frequently quoted in (<a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a>/<a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">s</a>)/Mpc, thus giving the speed in km/s of a galaxy 1 megaparsec (3.09<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>19</sup>&#160;km) away. The reciprocal of <i>H</i><sub>0</sub> is the <a href="#Hubble_time">Hubble time</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001735200000000000&#9824;</span>73.52<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>1.62</span>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(spacecraft)" title="Gaia (spacecraft)">Gaia</a>\n</td>\n<td><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gaiariess2018_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-gaiariess2018-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardianhubbleconstant_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardianhubbleconstant-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Additional HST <a href="/wiki/Photometry_(astronomy)" title="Photometry (astronomy)">photometry</a> of galactic <a href="/wiki/Cepheid_variable" title="Cepheid variable">Cepheids</a> with early Gaia <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a> measurements. The revised value increases tension with <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">CMB</a> measurements at the 3.8<a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">&#963;</a> level. Continuation of a collaboration known as Supernovae, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43910602a221b7a4c373791f94793e3008622070" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.985ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, for the Equation of State of Dark Energy (SHoES).\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2018-02-22\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001734500000000000&#9824;</span>73.45<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>1.66</span>\n</td>\n<td>Hubble Space Telescope\n</td>\n<td><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180222_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180222-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Parallax measurements of galactic Cepheids for enhanced calibration of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_distance_ladder" title="Cosmic distance ladder">distance ladder</a>; the value suggests a discrepancy with CMB measurements at the 3.7&#963; level. measurements, squares represent early universe CMB/BAO measurements with &#923;CDM parameters while triangles are independent measurements.</small></div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2550" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg/220px-Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg/330px-Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg/440px-Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_steps_to_the_Hubble_constant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three steps to the Hubble constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>A decade before Hubble made his observations, a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physicists" title="Physicists">physicists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematicians</a> had established a consistent theory of the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">space and time</a> by using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein%27s_field_equations" title="Einstein\'s field equations">Einstein\'s field equations</a> of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>. Applying the most <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang#Underlying_assumptions" title="Big Bang">general principles</a> to the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> yielded a <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)" title="Dynamics (mechanics)">dynamic</a> solution that conflicted with the then-prevailing notion of a <a href="/wiki/Static_universe" title="Static universe">static universe</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="FLRW_equations">FLRW equations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: FLRW equations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1922, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedman" title="Alexander Friedman">Alexander Friedmann</a> derived his <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations" title="Friedmann equations">Friedmann equations</a> from <a href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equations" title="Einstein field equations">Einstein\'s field equations</a>, showing that the universe might expand at a rate calculable by the equations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The parameter used by Friedmann is known today as the <a href="/wiki/Scale_factor_(cosmology)" title="Scale factor (cosmology)">scale factor</a> which can be considered as a <a href="/wiki/Scale_invariance" title="Scale invariance">scale invariant</a> form of the <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportionality constant</a> of Hubble\'s law. <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a> independently found a similar solution in 1927. The Friedmann equations are derived by inserting the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">metric for a homogeneous and isotropic universe</a> into Einstein\'s field equations for a fluid with a given <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>. This idea of an expanding spacetime would eventually lead to the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steady_State_Theory" title="Steady State Theory">Steady State</a> theories of cosmology.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Lema.C3.AEtre.27s_Equation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lema&#238;tre\'s_Equation">Lema&#238;tre\'s Equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lema&#238;tre\'s Equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1927, two years before Hubble published his own article, the Belgian priest and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a> was the first to publish research deriving what is now known as Hubble\'s Law. According to the Canadian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Sidney_van_den_Bergh" title="Sidney van den Bergh">Sidney van den Bergh</a>, "The 1927 discovery of the expansion of the universe by Lema&#238;tre was published in French in a low-impact journal. In the 1931 high-impact English translation of this article a critical equation was changed by omitting reference to what is now known as the Hubble constant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> It is now known that the alterations in the translated paper were carried out by Lemaitre himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Livio_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Livio-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shape_of_the_universe">Shape of the universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Shape of the universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before the advent of <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">modern cosmology</a>, there was considerable talk about the size and <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">shape</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>. In 1920, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Debate_(astronomy)" title="Great Debate (astronomy)">Shapley-Curtis debate</a> took place between <a href="/wiki/Harlow_Shapley" title="Harlow Shapley">Harlow Shapley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heber_Doust_Curtis" title="Heber Doust Curtis">Heber D. Curtis</a> over this issue. Shapley argued for a small universe the size of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_galaxy" title="Milky Way galaxy">Milky Way galaxy</a> and Curtis argued that the universe was much larger. The issue was resolved in the coming decade with Hubble\'s improved observations.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cepheid_variable_stars_outside_of_the_Milky_Way">Cepheid variable stars outside of the Milky Way</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cepheid variable stars outside of the Milky Way">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Edwin Hubble did most of his professional astronomical observing work at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson Observatory</a>, home to the world\'s most powerful telescope at the time. His observations of <a href="/wiki/Cepheid_variable" title="Cepheid variable">Cepheid variable</a> stars in &#8220;<i>spiral <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a></i>&#8221; enabled him to calculate the distances to these objects. Surprisingly, these objects were discovered to be at distances which placed them well outside the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>. They continued to be called &#8220;nebulae&#8221; and it was only gradually that the term &#8220;galaxies&#8221; replaced it.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combining_redshifts_with_distance_measurements">Combining redshifts with distance measurements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Combining redshifts with distance measurements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="box-Disputed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-disputed" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="System-search.svg" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="45" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/System-search.svg/45px-System-search.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/System-search.svg/68px-System-search.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/System-search.svg/90px-System-search.svg.png 2x" width="45"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section\'s <b>factual accuracy is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">disputed</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on <a href="/wiki/Talk:Hubble%27s_law#Dispute" title="Talk:Hubble\'s law">Talk:Hubble\'s law</a>. Please help to ensure that disputed statements are <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources">reliably sourced</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2013</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_constant.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="855" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Hubble_constant.JPG/250px-Hubble_constant.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Hubble_constant.JPG/375px-Hubble_constant.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Hubble_constant.JPG/500px-Hubble_constant.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_constant.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fit of <a href="#Redshift_velocity">redshift velocities</a> to Hubble\'s law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keel_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keel-61">[61]</a></sup> Various estimates for the Hubble constant exist. The HST Key <i>H</i><sub>0</sub> Group fitted type Ia supernovae for redshifts between 0.01 and 0.1 to find that <i>H</i><sub>0</sub> = 71 &#177; 2 (statistical) &#177; 6 (systematic) km s<sup>&#8722;1</sup>Mpc<sup>&#8722;1</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedman2001_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman2001-50">[50]</a></sup> while Sandage <i>et al.</i> find <i>H</i><sub>0</sub> = 62.3 &#177; 1.3 (statistical) &#177; 5 (systematic) km s<sup>&#8722;1</sup>Mpc<sup>&#8722;1</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberg_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberg-62">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The parameters that appear in Hubble\'s law, velocities and distances, are not directly measured. In reality we determine, say, a supernova brightness, which provides information about its distance, and the redshift <i>z</i> = &#8710;<i>&#955;</i>/<i>&#955;</i> of its spectrum of radiation. Hubble correlated brightness and parameter <i>z</i>.\n</p><p>Combining his measurements of galaxy distances with <a href="/wiki/Vesto_Slipher" title="Vesto Slipher">Vesto Slipher</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milton_Humason" title="Milton Humason">Milton Humason</a>\'s measurements of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redshifts" title="Redshifts">redshifts</a> associated with the galaxies, Hubble discovered a rough proportionality between redshift of an object and its distance. Though there was considerable <a href="/wiki/Variance" title="Variance">scatter</a> (now known to be caused by <a href="/wiki/Peculiar_velocity" title="Peculiar velocity">peculiar velocities</a>&#8212;the \'Hubble flow\' is used to refer to the region of space far enough out that the recession velocity is larger than local peculiar velocities), Hubble was able to plot a trend line from the 46 galaxies he studied and obtain a value for the Hubble constant of 500&#160;km/s/Mpc (much higher than the currently accepted value due to errors in his distance calibrations). (See <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_distance_ladder" title="Cosmic distance ladder">cosmic distance ladder</a> for details.)\n</p><p>At the time of discovery and development of Hubble\'s law, it was acceptable to explain redshift phenomenon as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> in the context of special relativity, and use the Doppler formula to associate redshift <i>z</i> with velocity. Today, in the context of general relativity, velocity between distant objects depends on the choice of coordinates used, and therefore, the redshift can be equally described as a Doppler shift or a cosmological shift (or gravitational) due to the expanding space, or some combination of the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hubble_Diagram">Hubble Diagram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hubble Diagram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Hubble\'s law can be easily depicted in a "Hubble Diagram" in which the velocity (assumed approximately proportional to the redshift) of an object is plotted with respect to its distance from the observer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> A straight line of positive slope on this diagram is the visual depiction of Hubble\'s law.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmological_constant_abandoned">Cosmological constant abandoned</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cosmological constant abandoned">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">Cosmological constant</a></div>\n<p>After Hubble\'s discovery was published, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> abandoned his work on the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>, which he had designed to modify his equations of general relativity to allow them to produce a static solution, which he thought was the correct state of the universe. The Einstein equations in their simplest form model generally either an expanding or contracting universe, so Einstein\'s cosmological constant was artificially created to counter the expansion or contraction to get a perfect static and flat universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mapcc_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-mapcc-65">[65]</a></sup> After Hubble\'s discovery that the universe was, in fact, expanding, Einstein called his faulty assumption that the universe is static his "biggest mistake".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mapcc_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-mapcc-65">[65]</a></sup> On its own, general relativity could predict the expansion of the universe, which (through <a href="/wiki/Tests_of_general_relativity" title="Tests of general relativity">observations</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">bending of light by large masses</a>, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion_precession_of_Mercury" title="Perihelion precession of Mercury">precession of the orbit of Mercury</a>) could be experimentally observed and compared to his theoretical calculations using particular solutions of the equations he had originally formulated.\n</p><p>In 1931, Einstein made a trip to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson</a> to thank Hubble for providing the observational basis for modern cosmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The cosmological constant has regained attention in recent decades as a hypothesis for <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p><span id="redshift"></span>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretation">Interpretation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Interpretation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Velocity-redshift.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="626" data-file-width="824" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Velocity-redshift.JPG/250px-Velocity-redshift.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Velocity-redshift.JPG/375px-Velocity-redshift.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Velocity-redshift.JPG/500px-Velocity-redshift.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Velocity-redshift.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A variety of possible recessional velocity vs. redshift functions including the simple linear relation <i>v</i> = <i>cz</i>; a variety of possible shapes from theories related to general relativity; and a curve that does not permit speeds faster than light in accordance with special relativity. All curves are linear at low redshifts. See Davis and Lineweaver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D&amp;L_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-D&amp;L-68">[68]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The discovery of the linear relationship between <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> and distance, coupled with a supposed linear relation between <a href="/wiki/Recessional_velocity" title="Recessional velocity">recessional velocity</a> and redshift, yields a straightforward mathematical expression for Hubble\'s law as follows:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v=H_{0}\\,D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v=H_{0}\\,D}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v=H_{0}\\,D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d7c164df8a15901f4d545126ca77df93ba65e765" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:9.523ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where\n</p>\n<ul><li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e07b00e7fc0847fbd16391c778d65bc25c452597" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.128ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the recessional velocity, typically expressed in km/s.</li>\n<li><i>H</i><sub>0</sub> is Hubble\'s constant and corresponds to the value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> (often termed the <b>Hubble parameter</b> which is a value that is <a href="/wiki/Time-variant_system" title="Time-variant system">time dependent</a> and which can be expressed in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Scale_factor_(cosmology)" title="Scale factor (cosmology)">scale factor</a>) in the Friedmann equations taken at the time of observation denoted by the subscript <i>0</i>. This value is the same throughout the universe for a given <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_time#Comoving_coordinates" title="Comoving time">comoving time</a>.</li>\n<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle D}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.924ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the proper distance (which can change over time, unlike the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" title="Comoving distance">comoving distance</a>, which is constant) from the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> to the observer, measured in <a href="/wiki/Mega-" title="Mega-">mega</a> <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> (Mpc), in the 3-space defined by given <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological_time" title="Cosmological time">cosmological time</a>. (Recession velocity is just <i>v</i> = <i>dD/dt</i>).</li></ul>\n<p>Hubble\'s law is considered a fundamental relation between recessional velocity and distance. However, the relation between recessional velocity and redshift depends on the cosmological model adopted and is not established except for small redshifts.\n</p><p>For distances <i>D</i> larger than the radius of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_sphere" title="Hubble sphere">Hubble sphere</a> <i>r</i><sub>HS</sub>&#160;, objects recede at a rate faster than the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> (<i>See</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance#Uses_of_the_proper_distance" title="Comoving distance">Uses of the proper distance</a> for a discussion of the significance of this):\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r_{HS}={\\frac {c}{H_{0}}}\\ .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mi>S</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mtext>&#160;</mtext>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r_{HS}={\\frac {c}{H_{0}}}\\ .}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="r_{{HS}}={\\frac  {c}{H_{0}}}\\ ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1ad4039cce5e6eeadb112538c2911e89c1456df1" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:11.948ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Since the Hubble "constant" is a constant only in space, not in time, the radius of the Hubble sphere may increase or decrease over various time intervals. The subscript \'0\' indicates the value of the Hubble constant today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keel_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keel-61">[61]</a></sup> Current evidence suggests that the expansion of the universe is accelerating (<i>see</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">Accelerating universe</a>), meaning that, for any given galaxy, the recession velocity dD/dt is increasing over time as the galaxy moves to greater and greater distances; however, the Hubble parameter is actually thought to be decreasing with time, meaning that if we were to look at some <i>fixed</i> distance D and watch a series of different galaxies pass that distance, later galaxies would pass that distance at a smaller velocity than earlier ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Redshift_velocity_and_recessional_velocity">Redshift velocity and recessional velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Redshift velocity and recessional velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">Redshift</a> can be measured by determining the wavelength of a known transition, such as hydrogen &#945;-lines for distant quasars, and finding the fractional shift compared to a stationary reference. Thus redshift is a quantity unambiguous for experimental observation. The relation of redshift to recessional velocity is another matter. For an extensive discussion, see Harrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Redshift_velocity">Redshift velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Redshift velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The redshift <i>z</i> is often described as a <i>redshift velocity</i>, which is the recessional velocity that would produce the same redshift <i>if</i> it were caused by a linear <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a> (which, however, is not the case, as the shift is caused in part by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">cosmological expansion of space</a>, and because the velocities involved are too large to use a non-relativistic formula for Doppler shift). This redshift velocity can easily exceed the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madsen_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madsen-71">[71]</a></sup> In other words, to determine the redshift velocity <i>v<sub>rs</sub></i>, the relation:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v_{rs}\\equiv cz,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mi>s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8801;<!-- &#8801; --></mo>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v_{rs}\\equiv cz,}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle v_{rs}\\equiv cz,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/341851f0ad277dce712460fb3b3416f9eb305873" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.713ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dekel_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dekel-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Padmanabhan_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Padmanabhan-73">[73]</a></sup> That is, there is <i>no fundamental difference</i> between redshift velocity and redshift: they are rigidly proportional, and not related by any theoretical reasoning. The motivation behind the "redshift velocity" terminology is that the redshift velocity agrees with the velocity from a low-velocity simplification of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_Doppler_effect" title="Relativistic Doppler effect">Fizeau-Doppler formula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sartori_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sartori-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {\\lambda _{o}}{\\lambda _{e}}}-1={\\sqrt {\\frac {1+{\\frac {v}{c}}}{1-{\\frac {v}{c}}}}}-1\\approx {\\frac {v}{c}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>o</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {\\lambda _{o}}{\\lambda _{e}}}-1={\\sqrt {\\frac {1+{\\frac {v}{c}}}{1-{\\frac {v}{c}}}}}-1\\approx {\\frac {v}{c}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {\\lambda _{o}}{\\lambda _{e}}}-1={\\sqrt {\\frac {1+{\\frac {v}{c}}}{1-{\\frac {v}{c}}}}}-1\\approx {\\frac {v}{c}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ac647240ca83af52d9166372ab3fcb2411466d2" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:33.017ex; height:7.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Here, <i>&#955;<sub>o</sub></i>, <i>&#955;<sub>e</sub></i> are the observed and emitted wavelengths respectively. The "redshift velocity" <i>v<sub>rs</sub></i> is not so simply related to real velocity at larger velocities, however, and this terminology leads to confusion if interpreted as a real velocity. Next, the connection between redshift or redshift velocity and recessional velocity is discussed. This discussion is based on Sartori.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L_Sartori_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-L_Sartori-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recessional_velocity">Recessional velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Recessional velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Suppose <i>R(t)</i> is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Scale_factor_(cosmology)" title="Scale factor (cosmology)">scale factor</a></i> of the universe, and increases as the universe expands in a manner that depends upon the <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmological model</a> selected. Its meaning is that all measured proper distances <i>D(t)</i> between co-moving points increase proportionally to <i>R</i>. (The co-moving points are not moving relative to each other except as a result of the expansion of space.) In other words:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {D(t)}{D(t_{0})}}={\\frac {R(t)}{R(t_{0})}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {D(t)}{D(t_{0})}}={\\frac {R(t)}{R(t_{0})}},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {D(t)}{D(t_{0})}}={\\frac {R(t)}{R(t_{0})}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d79ece78d57b7a6a14e3ff94133754b93a6a51f0" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:16.512ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>t</i><sub>0</sub> is some reference time. If light is emitted from a galaxy at time <i>t<sub>e</sub></i> and received by us at <i>t</i><sub>0</sub>, it is redshifted due to the expansion of space, and this redshift <i>z</i> is simply:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{e})}}-1.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1.</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{e})}}-1.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{e})}}-1.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e8bee9b7cb8a3a914b68b0c980792f728dda8e9b" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:15.14ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Suppose a galaxy is at distance <i>D</i>, and this distance changes with time at a rate <i>d<sub>t</sub>D</i>. We call this rate of recession the "recession velocity" <i>v<sub>r</sub></i>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v_{r}=d_{t}D={\\frac {d_{t}R}{R}}D.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v_{r}=d_{t}D={\\frac {d_{t}R}{R}}D.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle v_{r}=d_{t}D={\\frac {d_{t}R}{R}}D.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a4744562499073bd543a4e1ef937fa88b0c74b77" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:19.464ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>We now define the Hubble constant as\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H\\equiv {\\frac {d_{t}R}{R}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo>&#8801;<!-- &#8801; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H\\equiv {\\frac {d_{t}R}{R}},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle H\\equiv {\\frac {d_{t}R}{R}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0cb13fb57afa8286601828ec7c734aa8c92bdcce" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:10.444ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>and discover the Hubble law:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v_{r}=HD.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v_{r}=HD.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle v_{r}=HD.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5594aaf8a61e65972c2c3bfb6d47e88522f5321d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:9.835ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>From this perspective, Hubble\'s law is a fundamental relation between (i) the recessional velocity contributed by the expansion of space and (ii) the distance to an object; the connection between redshift and distance is a crutch used to connect Hubble\'s law with observations. This law can be related to redshift <i>z</i> approximately by making a <a href="/wiki/Taylor_series" title="Taylor series">Taylor series</a> expansion:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{e})}}-1\\approx {\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{0})\\left(1+(t_{e}-t_{0})H(t_{0})\\right)}}-1\\approx (t_{0}-t_{e})H(t_{0}),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{e})}}-1\\approx {\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{0})\\left(1+(t_{e}-t_{0})H(t_{0})\\right)}}-1\\approx (t_{0}-t_{e})H(t_{0}),}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle z={\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{e})}}-1\\approx {\\frac {R(t_{0})}{R(t_{0})\\left(1+(t_{e}-t_{0})H(t_{0})\\right)}}-1\\approx (t_{0}-t_{e})H(t_{0}),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bef15b8714a9350d497da09d548292fa25ddea92" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:66.139ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>If the distance is not too large, all other complications of the model become small corrections and the time interval is simply the distance divided by the speed of light:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle z\\approx (t_{0}-t_{e})H(t_{0})\\approx {\\frac {D}{c}}H(t_{0}),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle z\\approx (t_{0}-t_{e})H(t_{0})\\approx {\\frac {D}{c}}H(t_{0}),}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle z\\approx (t_{0}-t_{e})H(t_{0})\\approx {\\frac {D}{c}}H(t_{0}),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/223113d43d3d0518b34f12c743a93edf643d76a6" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:30.607ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>or\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle cz\\approx DH(t_{0})=v_{r}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle cz\\approx DH(t_{0})=v_{r}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle cz\\approx DH(t_{0})=v_{r}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/99770c6896da9817c8672e34069587c91b8923a8" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.731ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>According to this approach, the relation <i>cz</i> = <i>v<sub>r</sub></i> is an approximation valid at low redshifts, to be replaced by a relation at large redshifts that is model-dependent. See <a href="#redshift">velocity-redshift figure</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observability_of_parameters">Observability of parameters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Observability of parameters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Strictly speaking, neither <i>v</i> nor <i>D</i> in the formula are directly observable, because they are properties <i>now</i> of a galaxy, whereas our observations refer to the galaxy in the past, at the time that the light we currently see left it.\n</p><p>For relatively nearby galaxies (<a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> <i>z</i> much less than unity), <i>v</i> and <i>D</i> will not have changed much, and <i>v</i> can be estimated using the formula <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v=zc}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v=zc}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v=zc" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/082adc831f173204b48e93f534b239e6474d9f55" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.321ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> where <i>c</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. This gives the empirical relation found by Hubble.\n</p><p>For distant galaxies, <i>v</i> (or <i>D</i>) cannot be calculated from <i>z</i> without specifying a detailed model for how <i>H</i> changes with time. The redshift is not even directly related to the recession velocity at the time the light set out, but it does have a simple interpretation: <i>(1+z)</i> is the factor by which the universe has expanded while the photon was travelling towards the observer.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_velocity_vs_relative_velocity">Expansion velocity vs relative velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Expansion velocity vs relative velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In using Hubble\'s law to determine distances, only the velocity due to the expansion of the universe can be used. Since gravitationally interacting galaxies move relative to each other independent of the expansion of the universe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM-20170818_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM-20170818-77">[77]</a></sup> these relative velocities, called <a href="/wiki/Peculiar_velocity" title="Peculiar velocity">peculiar velocities</a>, need to be accounted for in the application of Hubble\'s law.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Redshift-space_distortions" title="Redshift-space distortions">Finger of God</a> effect is one result of this phenomenon. In <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">systems that are gravitationally bound</a>, such as galaxies or our planetary system, the expansion of space is a much weaker effect than the attractive force of gravity.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time-dependence_of_Hubble_parameter">Time-dependence of Hubble parameter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Time-dependence of Hubble parameter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The parameter <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is commonly called the &#8220;<i>Hubble constant</i>&#8221;, but that is a misnomer since it is constant in space only at a fixed time; it varies with time in nearly all cosmological models, and all observations of far distant objects are also observations into the distant past, when the &#8220;constant&#8221; had a different value. The &#8220;<i>Hubble parameter</i>&#8221; is a more correct term, with <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle H_{0}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43910602a221b7a4c373791f94793e3008622070" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.985ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> denoting the present-day value.\n</p><p>Another common source of confusion is that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">accelerating universe</a> does <i>not</i> imply that the Hubble parameter is actually increasing with time; since <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H(t)\\equiv {\\dot {a}}(t)/a(t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>&#8801;<!-- &#8801; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo>&#729;<!-- &#729; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H(t)\\equiv {\\dot {a}}(t)/a(t)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle H(t)\\equiv {\\dot {a}}(t)/a(t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d249ba1a079d982bf9459dcd81a006b1087ff858" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.731ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, in most accelerating models <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle a}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>a</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle a}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="a" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.23ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> increases relatively faster than <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\dot {a}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo>&#729;<!-- &#729; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\dot {a}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\dot {a}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c9167337d4bf7b72b9099d7d5714497cda8643ca" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.23ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, so H decreases with time. (The recession velocity of one chosen galaxy does increase, but different galaxies passing a sphere of fixed radius cross the sphere more slowly at later times.)\n</p><p>On defining the dimensionless <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">deceleration parameter</a>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle q\\equiv -{\\frac {{\\ddot {a}}\\,a}{{\\dot {a}}^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>q</mi>\n<mo>&#8801;<!-- &#8801; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo>&#168;<!-- &#168; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mi>a</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo>&#729;<!-- &#729; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle q\\equiv -{\\frac {{\\ddot {a}}\\,a}{{\\dot {a}}^{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle q\\equiv -{\\frac {{\\ddot {a}}\\,a}{{\\dot {a}}^{2}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4be133d70269fbf6c14cfe1077667150ee43c8e2" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:9.659ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span>, it follows that</dd>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {dH}{dt}}=-H^{2}(1+q)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>H</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>q</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {dH}{dt}}=-H^{2}(1+q)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {dH}{dt}}=-H^{2}(1+q)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6074f6135894d0c22f4a25365aae3a755f9624ba" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:19.062ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>From this it is seen that the Hubble parameter is decreasing with time, unless <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle q&lt;-1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>q</mi>\n<mo>&lt;</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle q&lt;-1}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle q&lt;-1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/11bb76dbc25dd5c71d6341ee15ace9745bbd004c" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.139ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>; the latter can only occur if the universe contains <a href="/wiki/Phantom_energy" title="Phantom energy">phantom energy</a>, regarded as theoretically somewhat improbable.\n</p><p>However, in the standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM" title="Lambda-CDM">Lambda-CDM</a> model, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>q</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle q}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06809d64fa7c817ffc7e323f85997f783dbdf71d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.07ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> will tend to &#8722;1 from above in the distant future as the cosmological constant becomes increasingly dominant over matter; this implies that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> will approach from above to a constant value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\approx 57}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mn>57</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\approx 57}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\approx 57}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/30298dab16f2ca27139b5a3503f4756db2f128fb" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:4.778ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> km/s/Mpc, and the scale factor of the universe will then grow exponentially in time.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Idealized_Hubble.27s_law"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Idealized_Hubble\'s_law">Idealized Hubble\'s law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Idealized Hubble\'s law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The mathematical derivation of an idealized Hubble\'s law for a uniformly expanding universe is a fairly elementary theorem of geometry in 3-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Cartesian</a>/Newtonian coordinate space, which, considered as a <a href="/wiki/Metric_space" title="Metric space">metric space</a>, is entirely <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_principle" title="Cosmological principle">homogeneous and isotropic</a> (properties do not vary with location or direction). Simply stated the theorem is this:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Any two points which are moving away from the origin, each along straight lines and with speed proportional to distance from the origin, will be moving away from each other with a speed proportional to their distance apart.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In fact this applies to non-Cartesian spaces as long as they are locally homogeneous and isotropic; specifically to the negatively and positively curved spaces frequently considered as cosmological models (see <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">shape of the universe</a>).\n</p><p>An observation stemming from this theorem is that seeing objects recede from us on Earth is not an indication that Earth is near to a center from which the expansion is occurring, but rather that <i>every</i> observer in an expanding universe will see objects receding from them.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultimate_fate_and_age_of_the_universe">Ultimate fate and age of the universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ultimate fate and age of the universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedmann_universes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Friedmann_universes.svg/400px-Friedmann_universes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Friedmann_universes.svg/600px-Friedmann_universes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Friedmann_universes.svg/800px-Friedmann_universes.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedmann_universes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_fate_of_the_universe" title="Ultimate fate of the universe">ultimate fate of the universe</a> can be determined by measuring the Hubble constant today and extrapolating with the observed value of the deceleration
